THE EARLY RACES (1950-1958)

The first Grand Prix was held on 13 May 1950 at Silverstone circuit and was won by Italian driver Giuseppe Farina, who also claimed the 1950 championship after beating his teammate, Argentine Juan Manuel Fangio (both driving for Alfa Romeo). However, Fangio went on to win the title in 1951, 1954, 1955, 1956 and 1957. His streak was interrupted by Ferrari driver Alberto Ascari's back-to-back championships. This era also saw drivers such as British Stirling Moss, who despite competing regularly was never able to win the World Championship, which is why the English consider him "the greatest driver never to have won a world title".

The period was dominated by teams run by car manufacturers — Alfa Romeo, Ferrari, Maserati and Mercedes-Benz — who had competed before the war. In the early seasons, pre-Second World War cars such as the Alfa Romeo 159 were still being used.

  Developed by Porsche, Honda and Mercedes-Benz, McLaren won sixteen championships during that period — seven constructors and nine drivers titles — while Williams used Ford, Honda and Renault engines to also win sixteen titles — nine constructors and seven drivers. The rivalry between drivers Ayrton Senna and Alain Prost became the central focus of F1 in 1988 and continued until the Frenchman retired at the end of 1993. Senna died at the 1994 San Marino Grand Prix after crashing into a wall exiting the Tamburello corner, the same weekend that Roland Ratzenberger lost his life in an accident during Saturday qualifying.


THE RETURN OF THE MANUFACTURERS

Michael Schumacher and Ferrari won five consecutive drivers championships (2000-2004) and six constructors championships (1999-2004), something without precedent. Schumacher set many new records, including those for Grand Prix victories (91), wins in a single season (thirteen of eighteen), and most drivers titles (seven). Schumacher's run ended on 25 September 2005, when Renault driver Fernando Alonso became the youngest Formula 1 champion at that time.

Grand Prix racing has its roots in motor racing events that originated in France in 1894. Initially they were individual events, unconnected and on dirt roads, with virtually no limitations. EXEC 3 | QUESTION 5: What is the function of the virtual safety car in Formula 1?
================================================================================

ANSWER:
The virtual safety car is used to neutralise the race in cases where it is necessary to show a double yellow flag in some area of the track due to the existence of a risk for competitors or track marshals, without the incident requiring the appearance of the safety car. [Source: The presence of the safety car on the track is indicated by a sign with the letters SC (from the English "safety car") accompanied by a yellow flag. Since 1995, Mercedes has been responsible for supplying this vehicle (with some exceptions such as the 1996 Argentine Grand Prix in which a Renault Clio was used). Since 2000, the driver in charge of driving it is former German DTM driver Bernd Mayländer. In the 2021 season, Mercedes shares the supply of the safety car with Aston Martin.]

In 2015, the use of the virtual safety car began to be implemented. It is used to neutralise the race in cases where it is necessary to show a double yellow flag in some area of the track due to the existence of a risk for competitors or track marshals, without the incident requiring the appearance of the safety car. When race control initiates the virtual safety car procedure, all luminous panels around the circuit will display the letters VSC and the teams will be notified. Drivers must respect a minimum time established by the FIA at least once at each control post.

The safety car is an organisation vehicle that reports directly to Race Control. SPORT AND REGULATIONS

The Formula 1 regulations, drawn up by the Fédération Internationale de l'Automobile, are divided into two main parts. The FIA Formula One World Championship, better known as Formula 1, F1 or Formula One, is the premier international motorsport competition and the most popular and prestigious motor racing championship in the world. It is governed by the Fédération Internationale de l'Automobile (FIA).   --- CHUNK 7 | Score: 0.335 ---
  The flags used in Formula 1 carry specific meanings that all drivers must understand. The green flag indicates that the danger has passed and overtaking can resume. It can also be shown during the warm-up lap, at the beginning of a simultaneous practice session at all marshal posts when the start is announced, upon ignition of the starting lights, or when a car enters the garage safely. The red flag signals the stoppage of all practice sessions or the race, whether due to an accident or meteorological conditions. All drivers must immediately reduce their speed, stop if necessary and return to the boxes or to the place provided for in the event regulations. Overtaking is prohibited when the red flag is shown. This flag is displayed solely by order of the race director. All timing screens throughout the circuit turn red. If it occurs at the start of the race or at the restart without completing the lap or at least the first sector, all drivers must return to their original positions from the last lap without a red flag. The yellow flag indicates danger and overtaking is not permitted, and drivers must reduce their speed.

  --- CHUNK 8 | Score: 0.323 ---

The development of electronic driver aids began during the 1980s. Lotus developed an active suspension system that first appeared during 1982 in the Lotus 91. In the early 1990s other teams followed suit and semi-automatic gearboxes and traction control became a natural progression. THE GARAGISTES (1950-1980)

The first major technological development occurred when the Cooper Car Company reintroduced rear-engined cars (following the pioneering Auto Union designs by Ferdinand Porsche in the 1930s), which evolved from the company's Formula 3 designs. Australian Jack Brabham, world champion in 1959, 1960 and 1966, soon demonstrated the superiority of the new design. By 1961, all regular competitors had switched to rear-engined cars. The Ferguson P99, with four-wheel drive, was the last front-engined Formula 1 car to participate in the World Championship, taking part only in the 1961 British Grand Prix.

The first British world champion was Mike Hawthorn, who drove a Ferrari to the title in 1958. However, when Colin Chapman entered Formula 1 as a chassis designer and later as founder of the Lotus Team, British drivers enjoyed many more victories in the following decade.

  In the early seasons, pre-Second World War cars such as the Alfa Romeo 159 were still being used. They featured front-mounted engines, either 1.5-litre supercharged or 4.5-litre naturally aspirated, and treaded tyres. The 1952 and 1953 World Championships were run under Formula 2 regulations, with smaller and less powerful cars, due to the limited number of available Formula 1 cars. When the Formula 1 World Championship was reinstated, engines were limited to 2.5 litres. In 1954, Mercedes introduced the advanced W196, which featured innovations such as desmodromic valve timing and fuel injection, as well as a streamlined bodywork. Mercedes won the drivers championship two consecutive years (1954 and 1955) before withdrawing from all motorsport competition following the Le Mans disaster in 1955.

  --- CHUNK 11 | Score: 0.218 ---
  THE GRAND PRIX EVENTS

Each year several Grand Prix events are organised in different parts of the world. In the 2022 season the number was 22 Grand Prix, and in 2019 it was 21, for example. Previously the championships were shorter and the number of Grand Prix has progressively increased. In the early eras, championships averaged around 10-11 Grand Prix. In the 1980s and 1990s, the number of Grand Prix was around 13-15.

The competition is held over a weekend lasting three days. On Friday (exceptionally Thursday in Monaco), there are two free practice sessions where drivers test and adapt their car to the circuit, both in terms of setup and tyres. On Saturday another practice session is held, followed by qualifying. On Sunday the race takes place. The single-seaters line up on the starting grid thirty minutes before the standard stipulated start time or 13:00 GMT, although occasionally schedules may vary, mainly for the convenience of broadcasting in Europe, especially when races are held in Asia or Oceania.

  --- CHUNK 12 | Score: 0.209 ---
  Developed by Porsche, Honda and Mercedes-Benz, McLaren won sixteen championships during that period — seven constructors and nine drivers titles — while Williams used Ford, Honda and Renault engines to also win sixteen titles — nine constructors and seven drivers. THE RETURN OF THE MANUFACTURERS

Michael Schumacher and Ferrari won five consecutive drivers championships (2000-2004) and six constructors championships (1999-2004), something without precedent. Schumacher set many new records, including those for Grand Prix victories (91), wins in a single season (thirteen of eighteen), and most drivers titles (seven). Schumacher's run ended on 25 September 2005, when Renault driver Fernando Alonso became the youngest Formula 1 champion at that time.

  --- CHUNK 13 | Score: 0.204 ---
  FOCA threatened to establish a rival series, boycotted a Grand Prix and FISA withdrew its sanction from races. The result was the 1981 Concorde Agreement, which guaranteed technical stability as teams would receive reasonable notice of new regulations.

The development of electronic driver aids began during the 1980s. Lotus developed an active suspension system that first appeared during 1982 in the Lotus 91. In 1987, this system was perfected and driven to victory by Ayrton Senna at that year's Monaco Grand Prix. In the early 1990s other teams followed suit and semi-automatic gearboxes and traction control became a natural progression. The FIA, due to complaints that technology was determining race results more than driver skill, banned many of these aids for the 1994 season.

On track, McLaren and Williams dominated the 1980s and 1990s. Developed by Porsche, Honda and Mercedes-Benz, McLaren won sixteen championships during that period — seven constructors and nine drivers titles — while Williams used Ford, Honda and Renault engines to also win sixteen titles — nine constructors and seven drivers.

  --- CHUNK 14 | Score: 0.200 ---
  Drivers must respect a minimum time established by the FIA at least once at each control post. Cars must not lap unnecessarily slowly, erratically or in a way that could be potentially dangerous. Anything that does not conform to those minimum times may be penalised. To end the virtual safety car period, teams will be notified and at any point between 10 and 15 seconds later the VSC signals on the panels will be switched off and green lights will be activated to restart the race immediately. 30 seconds later the green lights will be switched off.

This measure was applied following the death of Jules Bianchi at the 2014 Japanese Grand Prix, as he crashed into the recovery crane removing Adrian Sutil's single-seater at turn 7.


RACE AND QUALIFYING PENALTIES

Track marshals are responsible for penalising competitors. Penalties are usually carried out for actions such as: jumping the start, dangerous manoeuvres, ignoring flags, driving too fast in the pit lane, etc., depending on the severity or type of infringement.

  THE GRAND PRIX EVENTS

Each year several Grand Prix events are organised in different parts of the world. In the 2022 season the number was 22 Grand Prix, and in 2019 it was 21, for example. Previously the championships were shorter and the number of Grand Prix has progressively increased. In the early eras, championships averaged around 10-11 Grand Prix. In the 1980s and 1990s, the number of Grand Prix was around 13-15.

The competition is held over a weekend lasting three days. On Friday (exceptionally Thursday in Monaco), there are two free practice sessions where drivers test and adapt their car to the circuit, both in terms of setup and tyres. On Saturday another practice session is held, followed by qualifying. On Sunday the race takes place. The single-seaters line up on the starting grid thirty minutes before the standard stipulated start time or 13:00 GMT, although occasionally schedules may vary, mainly for the convenience of broadcasting in Europe, especially when races are held in Asia or Oceania.

  The beginning of modern Formula 1 dates back to 1950, when teams such as Ferrari, Alfa Romeo and Maserati participated. Some were replaced by new ones such as McLaren, Williams, Red Bull, and others that returned like Mercedes have won the Formula One World Constructors Championship multiple times. Drivers must hold an FIA Super Licence to compete, which is obtained through results in other championships.


BACKGROUND

Grand Prix racing has its roots in motor racing events that originated in France in 1894. Initially they were individual events, unconnected and on dirt roads, with virtually no limitations. From 1927 to 1934, the number of races considered Grand Prix grew to reach eighteen in 1934, the maximum before the Second World War.


CREATION OF THE CHAMPIONSHIP

In 1945, immediately after the Second World War, there were only four races. Before the Second World War, rules had been established for Grand Prix competitions, which both cars and drivers had to follow.

  --- CHUNK 3 | Score: 0.374 ---
  Before the Second World War, rules had been established for Grand Prix competitions, which both cars and drivers had to follow. These rules, collectively known as the Formula, were not finalised until 1947, when the former AIACR was reorganised and renamed the Fédération Internationale de l'Automobile, known by the acronym FIA.

With its headquarters in Paris, at the end of the 1949 season it announced that for 1950 it would unite several national Grand Prix events to create a World Drivers Championship, so in 1950 the first Formula 1 championship was held. For economic reasons, in 1952 and 1953 competition still took place with Formula 2 cars, and the calendar continued to include several races that were not considered Grand Prix until 1983. A points system was established and a total of seven races were recognised as eligible for the World Championship.

  --- CHUNK 4 | Score: 0.354 ---
  This was done to improve the spectacle, allowing cars to follow each other more closely. In addition, an engine freeze has been implemented, where only reliability improvements can be made until the changes that will take place in 2026.


SPORT AND REGULATIONS

The Formula 1 regulations, drawn up by the Fédération Internationale de l'Automobile, are divided into two main parts. One of them, the Technical Regulations, refers to the technical conditions of the single-seaters, while the other, the Sporting Regulations, focuses on the conditions under which events must be held, as well as the scoring and penalty rules.


SCORING SYSTEM AND CHAMPIONSHIP ORDER

Over the years, the points system for drivers and constructors has varied, but it has always awarded more points to finishers at the top than at the bottom, except between 1950 and 1959 when the driver who set the fastest lap of the race also scored a point.

The scoring system has evolved significantly throughout the history of the championship.

  --- CHUNK 5 | Score: 0.338 ---
  The number of wheels on each car is limited to four, with no possibility of having three axles or twin wheels. In the 1970s, the Tyrrell P34 had four front wheels with a diameter of 10 inches.

For 2024, dry-weather tyres were reduced to 5 compounds, from C1 to C5 (with C1 being the most durable and slowest compound, and C5 the fastest with the most wear), eliminating the C0. Three compounds are chosen for each grand prix depending on the type of asphalt, which are named hard (white), medium (yellow) and soft (red). The most durable of the three is the hard, and the fastest is the soft. The tyres available for wet conditions are the intermediates (green) and the extreme wet tyres (blue). In this year the mandatory tyre system in qualifying that was trialled in 2023 was eliminated.

  --- CHUNK 6 | Score: 0.330 ---
  From 2019 onwards, the points allocation remains the same as the 2010 to 2018 period, with 25 points for first place down to 1 point for tenth place, and an additional 1 point is once again awarded for the fastest lap of the race, provided the driver who sets it finishes in the top ten.

The current points allocation in a Grand Prix is distributed as follows: the race winner receives 25 points; second place, 18; third, 15; fourth, 12; fifth, 10; sixth, 8; seventh, 6; eighth, 4; ninth, 2; and tenth, 1 point.


SAFETY SYSTEMS

Flags in Formula 1, and by extension in motorsport, are essential as they are the way in which track marshals communicate with drivers. Drivers must know and respect the different flags. The minimum dimensions are 60 cm x 80 cm, except for the red flag and the chequered flag which must be at least 80 x 100 cm.

The flags used in Formula 1 carry specific meanings that all drivers must understand.

  FORMULA 1: THE PINNACLE OF MOTORSPORT

The FIA Formula One World Championship, better known as Formula 1, F1 or Formula One, is the premier international motorsport competition and the most popular and prestigious motor racing championship in the world. It is governed by the Fédération Internationale de l'Automobile (FIA). Since September 2016, following the acquisition of the Formula One Group, the American company Liberty Media has been responsible for managing and operating the championship.

Each race is called a Grand Prix and the tournament that groups them is the Formula One World Championship. Most of the racing circuits where the Grand Prix events are held are purpose-built racing tracks, although street circuits are also used, and road circuits were used in the past. The cars used are single-seaters featuring the latest available technology, always limited by technical regulations. Some improvements developed in Formula 1 have ended up being used in commercial vehicles, such as the disc brake.

The beginning of modern Formula 1 dates back to 1950, when teams such as Ferrari, Alfa Romeo and Maserati participated.

THE HYBRID ERA AND NEW OWNERS

In 2014, Formula 1 underwent a major regulatory change, replacing V8 engines with turbocharged 1.6-litre V6 units. The single-seaters now feature a hybrid power unit, adding an energy recovery system. Honda joined Mercedes, Ferrari and Renault in 2015 as an engine supplier to McLaren, making them the only four manufacturers of this era.

Lewis Hamilton and Mercedes dominated the beginning of the hybrid era. His teammate Nico Rosberg won in 2016, but from 2017 Hamilton won four more consecutive titles, equalling Michael Schumacher's record of 7 world titles. In 2021, one of the most intense title battles took place between Hamilton and Max Verstappen, a duel that the Dutchman would ultimately win.

  A points system was established and a total of seven races were recognised as eligible for the World Championship.


THE EARLY RACES (1950-1958)

The first Grand Prix was held on 13 May 1950 at Silverstone circuit and was won by Italian driver Giuseppe Farina, who also claimed the 1950 championship after beating his teammate, Argentine Juan Manuel Fangio (both driving for Alfa Romeo). However, Fangio went on to win the title in 1951, 1954, 1955, 1956 and 1957. His streak was interrupted by Ferrari driver Alberto Ascari's back-to-back championships. This era also saw drivers such as British Stirling Moss, who despite competing regularly was never able to win the World Championship, which is why the English consider him "the greatest driver never to have won a world title".

The period was dominated by teams run by car manufacturers — Alfa Romeo, Ferrari, Maserati and Mercedes-Benz — who had competed before the war. In the early seasons, pre-Second World War cars such as the Alfa Romeo 159 were still being used.

  --- CHUNK 11 | Score: 0.285 ---
  In 2021, one of the most intense title battles took place between Hamilton and Max Verstappen, a duel that the Dutchman would ultimately win.

The engine development and the 2017 regulatory change, which improved aerodynamic downforce, resulted in the single-seaters becoming the fastest in history.

The Formula One Group has been controlled since September 2016 by the American company Liberty Media, owned by John C. Malone. Bernie Ecclestone continued as chief executive of the company until 23 January 2017, after 39 years at the helm of Formula 1 business.


THE NEW ERA

After the pandemic, which delayed the biggest aerodynamic change in Formula 1 in 40 years from 2021 to 2022, it finally took place. The cars adopted the so-called ground effect, and many parts of the car were simplified, such as the wings, to reduce the turbulence each part produces and consequently the dirty air, which makes it harder for the following car to get close. This was done to improve the spectacle, allowing cars to follow each other more closely.

  --- CHUNK 13 | Score: 0.279 ---
  The scoring system has evolved significantly throughout the history of the championship. From 1950 to 1959, first place received 8 points, second place 6 points, third place 4 points, fourth place 3 points, fifth place 2 points, and an additional 1 point was awarded for the fastest lap of the race. During the 1960s, the points allocation remained the same as the previous decade for the top five positions, with first place earning 8 points, second 6 points, third 4 points, fourth 3 points and fifth 2 points, but with 1 point now awarded for sixth place, and the fastest lap bonus was removed. From 1961 to 1990, the structure remained largely the same, with 9 points for first place, 6 for second, 4 for third, 3 for fourth, 2 for fifth and 1 for sixth place. Between 1991 and 2002, first place was awarded 10 points, second place 6 points, third place 4 points, fourth place 3 points, fifth place 2 points and sixth place 1 point.

THE GARAGISTES (1950-1980)

The first major technological development occurred when the Cooper Car Company reintroduced rear-engined cars (following the pioneering Auto Union designs by Ferdinand Porsche in the 1930s), which evolved from the company's Formula 3 designs. Australian Jack Brabham, world champion in 1959, 1960 and 1966, soon demonstrated the superiority of the new design. By 1961, all regular competitors had switched to rear-engined cars. The Ferguson P99, with four-wheel drive, was the last front-engined Formula 1 car to participate in the World Championship, taking part only in the 1961 British Grand Prix.

The first British world champion was Mike Hawthorn, who drove a Ferrari to the title in 1958. However, when Colin Chapman entered Formula 1 as a chassis designer and later as founder of the Lotus Team, British drivers enjoyed many more victories in the following decade.

THE BIG BUSINESS (1981 ONWARDS)

From the 1970s, Bernie Ecclestone reorganised the management of Formula 1's commercial rights. Ecclestone is widely credited with transforming the sport into a multi-billion dollar business. When Ecclestone bought the Brabham team in 1971 he earned a seat in the Formula One Constructors Association (FOCA) and in 1978 became its president. Previously, circuit owners controlled team revenues and negotiated with each individually; however, Ecclestone convinced the teams to do so collectively through FOCA.

The formation of the Fédération Internationale du Sport Automobile (FISA) during 1979 triggered the FISA-FOCA dispute, during which FISA and its president Jean-Marie Balestre repeatedly clashed with FOCA over television revenues and technical regulations.

Senna died at the 1994 San Marino Grand Prix after crashing into a wall exiting the Tamburello corner, the same weekend that Roland Ratzenberger lost his life in an accident during Saturday qualifying.


THE RETURN OF THE MANUFACTURERS

Michael Schumacher and Ferrari won five consecutive drivers championships (2000-2004) and six constructors championships (1999-2004), something without precedent. Schumacher set many new records, including those for Grand Prix victories (91), wins in a single season (thirteen of eighteen), and most drivers titles (seven). Schumacher's run ended on 25 September 2005, when Renault driver Fernando Alonso became the youngest Formula 1 champion at that time.

  --- CHUNK 13 | Score: 0.237 ---
  Drivers must respect a minimum time established by the FIA at least once at each control post. Cars must not lap unnecessarily slowly, erratically or in a way that could be potentially dangerous. Anything that does not conform to those minimum times may be penalised. To end the virtual safety car period, teams will be notified and at any point between 10 and 15 seconds later the VSC signals on the panels will be switched off and green lights will be activated to restart the race immediately. 30 seconds later the green lights will be switched off.

This measure was applied following the death of Jules Bianchi at the 2014 Japanese Grand Prix, as he crashed into the recovery crane removing Adrian Sutil's single-seater at turn 7.


RACE AND QUALIFYING PENALTIES

Track marshals are responsible for penalising competitors. Penalties are usually carried out for actions such as: jumping the start, dangerous manoeuvres, ignoring flags, driving too fast in the pit lane, etc., depending on the severity or type of infringement.


THE GRAND PRIX EVENTS

Each year several Grand Prix events are organised in different parts of the world.

  --- CHUNK 14 | Score: 0.234 ---
  The number of wheels on each car is limited to four, with no possibility of having three axles or twin wheels. In the 1970s, the Tyrrell P34 had four front wheels with a diameter of 10 inches.

For 2024, dry-weather tyres were reduced to 5 compounds, from C1 to C5 (with C1 being the most durable and slowest compound, and C5 the fastest with the most wear), eliminating the C0. Three compounds are chosen for each grand prix depending on the type of asphalt, which are named hard (white), medium (yellow) and soft (red). The most durable of the three is the hard, and the fastest is the soft. The tyres available for wet conditions are the intermediates (green) and the extreme wet tyres (blue). In this year the mandatory tyre system in qualifying that was trialled in 2023 was eliminated.

  --- CHUNK 15 | Score: 0.229 ---
  The chequered black and white flag indicates the end of the race, or as the case may be, the end of each of the practice sessions of the competition.

Furthermore, since the 2009 British Grand Prix, the FIA Safety Light System has been regularly used, which represented a major advance as it is much easier for a driver to see a warning in the form of a light than in the form of a flag, especially in poor visibility conditions. Three-colour LEDs were added to the steering wheels of drivers — yellow, blue and red — which indicate whether any flag is being shown (yellow or red) or whether a blue flag is being shown to them (blue LED).

The safety car is an organisation vehicle that reports directly to Race Control. Its basic function is to neutralise races in order to group competitors together in the event of a serious incident or due to meteorological conditions. The presence of the safety car on the track is indicated by a sign with the letters SC (from the English "safety car") accompanied by a yellow flag.

THE HYBRID ERA AND NEW OWNERS

THE GARAGISTES (1950-1980)

The first major technological development occurred when the Cooper Car Company reintroduced rear-engined cars (following the pioneering Auto Union designs by Ferdinand Porsche in the 1930s), which evolved from the company's Formula 3 designs. Australian Jack Brabham, world champion in 1959, 1960 and 1966, soon demonstrated the superiority of the new design. By 1961, all regular competitors had switched to rear-engined cars. The Ferguson P99, with four-wheel drive, was the last front-engined Formula 1 car to participate in the World Championship, taking part only in the 1961 British Grand Prix.

The first British world champion was Mike Hawthorn, who drove a Ferrari to the title in 1958. However, when Colin Chapman entered Formula 1 as a chassis designer and later as founder of the Lotus Team, British drivers enjoyed many more victories in the following decade.

BACKGROUND

Grand Prix racing has its roots in motor racing events that originated in France in 1894. Initially they were individual events, unconnected and on dirt roads, with virtually no limitations. From 1927 to 1934, the number of races considered Grand Prix grew to reach eighteen in 1934, the maximum before the Second World War.


CREATION OF THE CHAMPIONSHIP

THE NEW ERA

The cars adopted the so-called ground effect, and many parts of the car were simplified, such as the wings, to reduce the turbulence each part produces and consequently the dirty air, which makes it harder for the following car to get close. This was done to improve the spectacle, allowing cars to follow each other more closely.

  FORMULA 1: THE PINNACLE OF MOTORSPORT

The FIA Formula One World Championship, better known as Formula 1, F1 or Formula One, is the premier international motorsport competition and the most popular and prestigious motor racing championship in the world. It is governed by the Fédération Internationale de l'Automobile (FIA). Since September 2016, following the acquisition of the Formula One Group, the American company Liberty Media has been responsible for managing and operating the championship.

Each race is called a Grand Prix and the tournament that groups them is the Formula One World Championship. Most of the racing circuits where the Grand Prix events are held are purpose-built racing tracks, although street circuits are also used, and road circuits were used in the past. The cars used are single-seaters featuring the latest available technology, always limited by technical regulations. Some improvements developed in Formula 1 have ended up being used in commercial vehicles, such as the disc brake.

The beginning of modern Formula 1 dates back to 1950, when teams such as Ferrari, Alfa Romeo and Maserati participated.

  --- CHUNK 8 | Score: 0.315 ---

THE EARLY RACES (1950-1958)

The first Grand Prix was held on 13 May 1950 at Silverstone circuit and was won by Italian driver Giuseppe Farina, who also claimed the 1950 championship after beating his teammate, Argentine Juan Manuel Fangio (both driving for Alfa Romeo). However, Fangio went on to win the title in 1951, 1954, 1955, 1956 and 1957. His streak was interrupted by Ferrari driver Alberto Ascari's back-to-back championships. This era also saw drivers such as British Stirling Moss, who despite competing regularly was never able to win the World Championship, which is why the English consider him "the greatest driver never to have won a world title".

The period was dominated by teams run by car manufacturers — Alfa Romeo, Ferrari, Maserati and Mercedes-Benz — who had competed before the war. In the early seasons, pre-Second World War cars such as the Alfa Romeo 159 were still being used.

  --- CHUNK 9 | Score: 0.305 ---
  FOCA threatened to establish a rival series, boycotted a Grand Prix and FISA withdrew its sanction from races. The result was the 1981 Concorde Agreement, which guaranteed technical stability as teams would receive reasonable notice of new regulations.

The development of electronic driver aids began during the 1980s. Lotus developed an active suspension system that first appeared during 1982 in the Lotus 91. In 1987, this system was perfected and driven to victory by Ayrton Senna at that year's Monaco Grand Prix. In the early 1990s other teams followed suit and semi-automatic gearboxes and traction control became a natural progression. The FIA, due to complaints that technology was determining race results more than driver skill, banned many of these aids for the 1994 season.

On track, McLaren and Williams dominated the 1980s and 1990s. Developed by Porsche, Honda and Mercedes-Benz, McLaren won sixteen championships during that period — seven constructors and nine drivers titles — while Williams used Ford, Honda and Renault engines to also win sixteen titles — nine constructors and seven drivers.

  However, when Colin Chapman entered Formula 1 as a chassis designer and later as founder of the Lotus Team, British drivers enjoyed many more victories in the following decade. In 1962, Lotus introduced a car with an aluminium monocoque chassis instead of the traditional tubular frame. This proved to be the greatest technological advance since the introduction of rear-engined cars. In 1968, Lotus placed the Imperial Tobacco logo on their cars, introducing tobacco sponsorship to the sport.

Aerodynamics slowly gained importance in car design from the appearance of aerodynamic wings in the late 1960s. Towards the end of the following decade, Lotus introduced a ground-effect bodywork that generated enormous downforce and allowed cars to travel at higher speeds in corners — although the concept had already been used in Jim Hall's Chaparral 2J in 1970.

SPORT AND REGULATIONS

The Formula 1 regulations, drawn up by the Fédération Internationale de l'Automobile, are divided into two main parts. One of them, the Technical Regulations, refers to the technical conditions of the single-seaters, while the other, the Sporting Regulations, focuses on the conditions under which events must be held, as well as the scoring and penalty rules.


SCORING SYSTEM AND CHAMPIONSHIP ORDER

Over the years, the points system for drivers and constructors has varied, but it has always awarded more points to finishers at the top than at the bottom, except between 1950 and 1959 when the driver who set the fastest lap of the race also scored a point.

The scoring system has evolved significantly throughout the history of the championship.

  --- CHUNK 13 | Score: 0.286 ---

THE BIG BUSINESS (1981 ONWARDS)

From the 1970s, Bernie Ecclestone reorganised the management of Formula 1's commercial rights. Ecclestone is widely credited with transforming the sport into a multi-billion dollar business. When Ecclestone bought the Brabham team in 1971 he earned a seat in the Formula One Constructors Association (FOCA) and in 1978 became its president. Previously, circuit owners controlled team revenues and negotiated with each individually; however, Ecclestone convinced the teams to do so collectively through FOCA.

The formation of the Fédération Internationale du Sport Automobile (FISA) during 1979 triggered the FISA-FOCA dispute, during which FISA and its president Jean-Marie Balestre repeatedly clashed with FOCA over television revenues and technical regulations.

  --- CHUNK 14 | Score: 0.274 ---
  In the early seasons, pre-Second World War cars such as the Alfa Romeo 159 were still being used. They featured front-mounted engines, either 1.5-litre supercharged or 4.5-litre naturally aspirated, and treaded tyres. The 1952 and 1953 World Championships were run under Formula 2 regulations, with smaller and less powerful cars, due to the limited number of available Formula 1 cars. When the Formula 1 World Championship was reinstated, engines were limited to 2.5 litres. In 1954, Mercedes introduced the advanced W196, which featured innovations such as desmodromic valve timing and fuel injection, as well as a streamlined bodywork. Mercedes won the drivers championship two consecutive years (1954 and 1955) before withdrawing from all motorsport competition following the Le Mans disaster in 1955.

  --- CHUNK 15 | Score: 0.259 ---
  The flags used in Formula 1 carry specific meanings that all drivers must understand. The green flag indicates that the danger has passed and overtaking can resume. It can also be shown during the warm-up lap, at the beginning of a simultaneous practice session at all marshal posts when the start is announced, upon ignition of the starting lights, or when a car enters the garage safely. The red flag signals the stoppage of all practice sessions or the race, whether due to an accident or meteorological conditions. All drivers must immediately reduce their speed, stop if necessary and return to the boxes or to the place provided for in the event regulations. Overtaking is prohibited when the red flag is shown. This flag is displayed solely by order of the race director. All timing screens throughout the circuit turn red. If it occurs at the start of the race or at the restart without completing the lap or at least the first sector, all drivers must return to their original positions from the last lap without a red flag. The yellow flag indicates danger and overtaking is not permitted, and drivers must reduce their speed.

  THE STEERING WHEEL

Due to the limited space in the cockpit of a Formula 1 single-seater and the degree of concentration and attention required to drive a Formula 1 car, the steering wheel of one of these single-seaters is not only the control mechanism for the car's steering, but also a complex interface with multiple electronic devices, both for providing information to the driver via displays, and for controlling the vehicle via buttons and dials that serve various functions.


TYRES

Tyres are designed to withstand forces much greater than conventional ones, so they are manufactured with nylon, polyester fibre and soft rubber compounds. During the race a tyre can withstand more than one tonne of aerodynamic load, lateral forces of 4g and longitudinal forces of up to 5g. The number of wheels on each car is limited to four, with no possibility of having three axles or twin wheels. In the 1970s, the Tyrrell P34 had four front wheels with a diameter of 10 inches.

SPORT AND REGULATIONS

The Formula 1 regulations, drawn up by the Fédération Internationale de l'Automobile, are divided into two main parts. One of them, the Technical Regulations, refers to the technical conditions of the single-seaters, while the other, the Sporting Regulations, focuses on the conditions under which events must be held, as well as the scoring and penalty rules.


SCORING SYSTEM AND CHAMPIONSHIP ORDER

Over the years, the points system for drivers and constructors has varied, but it has always awarded more points to finishers at the top than at the bottom, except between 1950 and 1959 when the driver who set the fastest lap of the race also scored a point.

The scoring system has evolved significantly throughout the history of the championship.

  --- CHUNK 3 | Score: 0.424 ---
  The scoring system has evolved significantly throughout the history of the championship. From 1950 to 1959, first place received 8 points, second place 6 points, third place 4 points, fourth place 3 points, fifth place 2 points, and an additional 1 point was awarded for the fastest lap of the race. During the 1960s, the points allocation remained the same as the previous decade for the top five positions, with first place earning 8 points, second 6 points, third 4 points, fourth 3 points and fifth 2 points, but with 1 point now awarded for sixth place, and the fastest lap bonus was removed. From 1961 to 1990, the structure remained largely the same, with 9 points for first place, 6 for second, 4 for third, 3 for fourth, 2 for fifth and 1 for sixth place. Between 1991 and 2002, first place was awarded 10 points, second place 6 points, third place 4 points, fourth place 3 points, fifth place 2 points and sixth place 1 point.

  --- CHUNK 4 | Score: 0.385 ---
  Between 1991 and 2002, first place was awarded 10 points, second place 6 points, third place 4 points, fourth place 3 points, fifth place 2 points and sixth place 1 point. From 2003 to 2009, the system expanded to reward more positions, with first place receiving 10 points, second place 8 points, third place 6 points, fourth place 5 points, fifth place 4 points, sixth place 3 points, seventh place 2 points and eighth place 1 point. From 2010 to 2018, the current expanded system was introduced, awarding 25 points for first place, 18 for second, 15 for third, 12 for fourth, 10 for fifth, 8 for sixth, 6 for seventh, 4 for eighth, 2 for ninth and 1 point for tenth place.

  --- CHUNK 5 | Score: 0.363 ---
  Before the Second World War, rules had been established for Grand Prix competitions, which both cars and drivers had to follow. These rules, collectively known as the Formula, were not finalised until 1947, when the former AIACR was reorganised and renamed the Fédération Internationale de l'Automobile, known by the acronym FIA.

With its headquarters in Paris, at the end of the 1949 season it announced that for 1950 it would unite several national Grand Prix events to create a World Drivers Championship, so in 1950 the first Formula 1 championship was held. For economic reasons, in 1952 and 1953 competition still took place with Formula 2 cars, and the calendar continued to include several races that were not considered Grand Prix until 1983. A points system was established and a total of seven races were recognised as eligible for the World Championship.

  FORMULA 1: THE PINNACLE OF MOTORSPORT

The FIA Formula One World Championship, better known as Formula 1, F1 or Formula One, is the premier international motorsport competition and the most popular and prestigious motor racing championship in the world. It is governed by the Fédération Internationale de l'Automobile (FIA). Since September 2016, following the acquisition of the Formula One Group, the American company Liberty Media has been responsible for managing and operating the championship.

Each race is called a Grand Prix and the tournament that groups them is the Formula One World Championship. Most of the racing circuits where the Grand Prix events are held are purpose-built racing tracks, although street circuits are also used, and road circuits were used in the past. The cars used are single-seaters featuring the latest available technology, always limited by technical regulations. Some improvements developed in Formula 1 have ended up being used in commercial vehicles, such as the disc brake.

The beginning of modern Formula 1 dates back to 1950, when teams such as Ferrari, Alfa Romeo and Maserati participated.

Drivers must hold an FIA Super Licence to compete, which is obtained through results in other championships.


BACKGROUND

Grand Prix racing has its roots in motor racing events that originated in France in 1894. Initially they were individual events, unconnected and on dirt roads, with virtually no limitations.   THE GRAND PRIX EVENTS

Each year several Grand Prix events are organised in different parts of the world. In the 2022 season the number was 22 Grand Prix, and in 2019 it was 21, for example. Previously the championships were shorter and the number of Grand Prix has progressively increased. In the early eras, championships averaged around 10-11 Grand Prix. In the 1980s and 1990s, the number of Grand Prix was around 13-15.

The competition is held over a weekend lasting three days. On Friday (exceptionally Thursday in Monaco), there are two free practice sessions where drivers test and adapt their car to the circuit, both in terms of setup and tyres. On Saturday another practice session is held, followed by qualifying. On Sunday the race takes place. The single-seaters line up on the starting grid thirty minutes before the standard stipulated start time or 13:00 GMT, although occasionally schedules may vary, mainly for the convenience of broadcasting in Europe, especially when races are held in Asia or Oceania.

THE EARLY RACES (1950-1958)

The first Grand Prix was held on 13 May 1950 at Silverstone circuit and was won by Italian driver Giuseppe Farina, who also claimed the 1950 championship after beating his teammate, Argentine Juan Manuel Fangio (both driving for Alfa Romeo). However, Fangio went on to win the title in 1951, 1954, 1955, 1956 and 1957. His streak was interrupted by Ferrari driver Alberto Ascari's back-to-back championships. This era also saw drivers such as British Stirling Moss, who despite competing regularly was never able to win the World Championship, which is why the English consider him "the greatest driver never to have won a world title".

The period was dominated by teams run by car manufacturers — Alfa Romeo, Ferrari, Maserati and Mercedes-Benz — who had competed before the war. In the early seasons, pre-Second World War cars such as the Alfa Romeo 159 were still being used.

  --- CHUNK 11 | Score: 0.299 ---
  The flags used in Formula 1 carry specific meanings that all drivers must understand. The green flag indicates that the danger has passed and overtaking can resume. It can also be shown during the warm-up lap, at the beginning of a simultaneous practice session at all marshal posts when the start is announced, upon ignition of the starting lights, or when a car enters the garage safely. The red flag signals the stoppage of all practice sessions or the race, whether due to an accident or meteorological conditions. All drivers must immediately reduce their speed, stop if necessary and return to the boxes or to the place provided for in the event regulations. Overtaking is prohibited when the red flag is shown. This flag is displayed solely by order of the race director. All timing screens throughout the circuit turn red. If it occurs at the start of the race or at the restart without completing the lap or at least the first sector, all drivers must return to their original positions from the last lap without a red flag. The yellow flag indicates danger and overtaking is not permitted, and drivers must reduce their speed.

THE HYBRID ERA AND NEW OWNERS

In 2014, Formula 1 underwent a major regulatory change, replacing V8 engines with turbocharged 1.6-litre V6 units. The single-seaters now feature a hybrid power unit, adding an energy recovery system. Honda joined Mercedes, Ferrari and Renault in 2015 as an engine supplier to McLaren, making them the only four manufacturers of this era.

Lewis Hamilton and Mercedes dominated the beginning of the hybrid era. His teammate Nico Rosberg won in 2016, but from 2017 Hamilton won four more consecutive titles, equalling Michael Schumacher's record of 7 world titles. In 2021, one of the most intense title battles took place between Hamilton and Max Verstappen, a duel that the Dutchman would ultimately win.

  THE STEERING WHEEL

Due to the limited space in the cockpit of a Formula 1 single-seater and the degree of concentration and attention required to drive a Formula 1 car, the steering wheel of one of these single-seaters is not only the control mechanism for the car's steering, but also a complex interface with multiple electronic devices, both for providing information to the driver via displays, and for controlling the vehicle via buttons and dials that serve various functions.


TYRES

Tyres are designed to withstand forces much greater than conventional ones, so they are manufactured with nylon, polyester fibre and soft rubber compounds. During the race a tyre can withstand more than one tonne of aerodynamic load, lateral forces of 4g and longitudinal forces of up to 5g. The number of wheels on each car is limited to four, with no possibility of having three axles or twin wheels. In the 1970s, the Tyrrell P34 had four front wheels with a diameter of 10 inches.

THE GARAGISTES (1950-1980)

The first major technological development occurred when the Cooper Car Company reintroduced rear-engined cars (following the pioneering Auto Union designs by Ferdinand Porsche in the 1930s), which evolved from the company's Formula 3 designs. Australian Jack Brabham, world champion in 1959, 1960 and 1966, soon demonstrated the superiority of the new design. By 1961, all regular competitors had switched to rear-engined cars. The Ferguson P99, with four-wheel drive, was the last front-engined Formula 1 car to participate in the World Championship, taking part only in the 1961 British Grand Prix.

The first British world champion was Mike Hawthorn, who drove a Ferrari to the title in 1958. However, when Colin Chapman entered Formula 1 as a chassis designer and later as founder of the Lotus Team, British drivers enjoyed many more victories in the following decade.

The development of electronic driver aids began during the 1980s. Lotus developed an active suspension system that first appeared during 1982 in the Lotus 91. THE BIG BUSINESS (1981 ONWARDS)

From the 1970s, Bernie Ecclestone reorganised the management of Formula 1's commercial rights. Ecclestone is widely credited with transforming the sport into a multi-billion dollar business. When Ecclestone bought the Brabham team in 1971 he earned a seat in the Formula One Constructors Association (FOCA) and in 1978 became its president. Previously, circuit owners controlled team revenues and negotiated with each individually; however, Ecclestone convinced the teams to do so collectively through FOCA.

The formation of the Fédération Internationale du Sport Automobile (FISA) during 1979 triggered the FISA-FOCA dispute, during which FISA and its president Jean-Marie Balestre repeatedly clashed with FOCA over television revenues and technical regulations.

When the Formula 1 World Championship was reinstated, engines were limited to 2.5 litres. In 1954, Mercedes introduced the advanced W196, which featured innovations such as desmodromic valve timing and fuel injection, as well as a streamlined bodywork. EXEC 4 | QUESTION 3: What does a yellow flag with red stripes indicate in Formula 1?
================================================================================

ANSWER:
It indicates the existence on the track of an element that causes a reduction in grip. It can be shown due to oil residues or the presence of car fragments on the track, it is also shown in those areas of the circuit where the track is dry and it is beginning to rain. Drivers should reduce their speed at that point. [Source: It can be shown due to oil residues or the presence of car fragments on the track, it is also shown in those areas of the circuit where the track is dry and it is beginning to rain.]

  --- CHUNK 1 | Score: 0.555 ---
  The flags used in Formula 1 carry specific meanings that all drivers must understand. The green flag indicates that the danger has passed and overtaking can resume. It can also be shown during the warm-up lap, at the beginning of a simultaneous practice session at all marshal posts when the start is announced, upon ignition of the starting lights, or when a car enters the garage safely. The red flag signals the stoppage of all practice sessions or the race, whether due to an accident or meteorological conditions. All drivers must immediately reduce their speed, stop if necessary and return to the boxes or to the place provided for in the event regulations. Overtaking is prohibited when the red flag is shown. This flag is displayed solely by order of the race director. All timing screens throughout the circuit turn red. If it occurs at the start of the race or at the restart without completing the lap or at least the first sector, all drivers must return to their original positions from the last lap without a red flag. The yellow flag indicates danger and overtaking is not permitted, and drivers must reduce their speed.

  --- CHUNK 2 | Score: 0.502 ---
  The driver has performed an unsporting manoeuvre and receives this sanction. It is shown once and if the competitor repeats the offence, the black flag will be shown. It is also used when a driver has exceeded the track limits three times in a race, to warn them that the next time there will be a time penalty. The yellow flag with red stripes, also known as the striped flag, indicates the existence on the track of an element that causes a reduction in grip. It can be shown due to oil residues or the presence of car fragments on the track; it is also shown in those areas of the circuit where the track is dry and it is beginning to rain. Drivers should reduce their speed at that point. The black flag with an orange circle, shown statically alongside the car number, indicates to the driver that their vehicle has mechanical problems that could constitute a danger to other competitors and to themselves, so they should stop in their box as soon as possible. Currently it is not widely used, as in these situations drivers are shown the information on the car screens if they are not segmented, and the driver is usually informed by radio.

  --- CHUNK 3 | Score: 0.428 ---
  The yellow flag indicates danger and overtaking is not permitted, and drivers must reduce their speed. It can be shown to drivers in two different ways: a single yellow flag means reduce speed, do not overtake and be prepared to change your line due to the presence of a danger on the edge of the track or on part of it; two yellow flags mean reduce speed, do not overtake and be prepared to change your line or even stop due to the presence of a danger that obstructs the track totally or partially. They are normally shown at the marshal post immediately before the danger, although in some cases more than one can be shown. The presence of this flag before the start, due to the impossibility of a driver starting, leads to the cancellation of the start. The safety car flag, which is a yellow flag with the letters SC or with a VSC marking on the panels, indicates that the safety car is intervening on the track. Drivers must reduce speed, not overtake, and even be prepared to stop and change their line as a danger totally or partially obstructs the track.

  --- CHUNK 4 | Score: 0.428 ---
  The chequered black and white flag indicates the end of the race, or as the case may be, the end of each of the practice sessions of the competition.

Furthermore, since the 2009 British Grand Prix, the FIA Safety Light System has been regularly used, which represented a major advance as it is much easier for a driver to see a warning in the form of a light than in the form of a flag, especially in poor visibility conditions. Three-colour LEDs were added to the steering wheels of drivers — yellow, blue and red — which indicate whether any flag is being shown (yellow or red) or whether a blue flag is being shown to them (blue LED).

The safety car is an organisation vehicle that reports directly to Race Control. Its basic function is to neutralise races in order to group competitors together in the event of a serious incident or due to meteorological conditions. The presence of the safety car on the track is indicated by a sign with the letters SC (from the English "safety car") accompanied by a yellow flag.

The current points allocation in a Grand Prix is distributed as follows: the race winner receives 25 points; second place, 18; third, 15; fourth, 12; fifth, 10; sixth, 8; seventh, 6; eighth, 4; ninth, 2; and tenth, 1 point.


SAFETY SYSTEMS

Flags in Formula 1, and by extension in motorsport, are essential as they are the way in which track marshals communicate with drivers. Drivers must know and respect the different flags. The minimum dimensions are 60 cm x 80 cm, except for the red flag and the chequered flag which must be at least 80 x 100 cm.

The flags used in Formula 1 carry specific meanings that all drivers must understand.

SPORT AND REGULATIONS

The Formula 1 regulations, drawn up by the Fédération Internationale de l'Automobile, are divided into two main parts. One of them, the Technical Regulations, refers to the technical conditions of the single-seaters, while the other, the Sporting Regulations, focuses on the conditions under which events must be held, as well as the scoring and penalty rules.


SCORING SYSTEM AND CHAMPIONSHIP ORDER

Over the years, the points system for drivers and constructors has varied, but it has always awarded more points to finishers at the top than at the bottom, except between 1950 and 1959 when the driver who set the fastest lap of the race also scored a point.

The scoring system has evolved significantly throughout the history of the championship.

  FORMULA 1: THE PINNACLE OF MOTORSPORT

The FIA Formula One World Championship, better known as Formula 1, F1 or Formula One, is the premier international motorsport competition and the most popular and prestigious motor racing championship in the world. It is governed by the Fédération Internationale de l'Automobile (FIA). Since September 2016, following the acquisition of the Formula One Group, the American company Liberty Media has been responsible for managing and operating the championship.

Each race is called a Grand Prix and the tournament that groups them is the Formula One World Championship. Most of the racing circuits where the Grand Prix events are held are purpose-built racing tracks, although street circuits are also used, and road circuits were used in the past. The cars used are single-seaters featuring the latest available technology, always limited by technical regulations. Some improvements developed in Formula 1 have ended up being used in commercial vehicles, such as the disc brake.

The beginning of modern Formula 1 dates back to 1950, when teams such as Ferrari, Alfa Romeo and Maserati participated.

  --- CHUNK 10 | Score: 0.303 ---
  The presence of the safety car on the track is indicated by a sign with the letters SC (from the English "safety car") accompanied by a yellow flag. Since 1995, Mercedes has been responsible for supplying this vehicle (with some exceptions such as the 1996 Argentine Grand Prix in which a Renault Clio was used). Since 2000, the driver in charge of driving it is former German DTM driver Bernd Mayländer. In the 2021 season, Mercedes shares the supply of the safety car with Aston Martin.

In 2015, the use of the virtual safety car began to be implemented. It is used to neutralise the race in cases where it is necessary to show a double yellow flag in some area of the track due to the existence of a risk for competitors or track marshals, without the incident requiring the appearance of the safety car. When race control initiates the virtual safety car procedure, all luminous panels around the circuit will display the letters VSC and the teams will be notified. Drivers must respect a minimum time established by the FIA at least once at each control post.

Drivers must hold an FIA Super Licence to compete, which is obtained through results in other championships.


BACKGROUND

Grand Prix racing has its roots in motor racing events that originated in France in 1894. Initially they were individual events, unconnected and on dirt roads, with virtually no limitations. From 1927 to 1934, the number of races considered Grand Prix grew to reach eighteen in 1934, the maximum before the Second World War.


CREATION OF THE CHAMPIONSHIP

In 1945, immediately after the Second World War, there were only four races. Before the Second World War, rules had been established for Grand Prix competitions, which both cars and drivers had to follow.

  --- CHUNK 12 | Score: 0.292 ---
  Before the Second World War, rules had been established for Grand Prix competitions, which both cars and drivers had to follow. These rules, collectively known as the Formula, were not finalised until 1947, when the former AIACR was reorganised and renamed the Fédération Internationale de l'Automobile, known by the acronym FIA.

With its headquarters in Paris, at the end of the 1949 season it announced that for 1950 it would unite several national Grand Prix events to create a World Drivers Championship, so in 1950 the first Formula 1 championship was held. For economic reasons, in 1952 and 1953 competition still took place with Formula 2 cars, and the calendar continued to include several races that were not considered Grand Prix until 1983. A points system was established and a total of seven races were recognised as eligible for the World Championship.

  --- CHUNK 13 | Score: 0.289 ---
  THE STEERING WHEEL

Due to the limited space in the cockpit of a Formula 1 single-seater and the degree of concentration and attention required to drive a Formula 1 car, the steering wheel of one of these single-seaters is not only the control mechanism for the car's steering, but also a complex interface with multiple electronic devices, both for providing information to the driver via displays, and for controlling the vehicle via buttons and dials that serve various functions.


TYRES

Tyres are designed to withstand forces much greater than conventional ones, so they are manufactured with nylon, polyester fibre and soft rubber compounds. During the race a tyre can withstand more than one tonne of aerodynamic load, lateral forces of 4g and longitudinal forces of up to 5g. The number of wheels on each car is limited to four, with no possibility of having three axles or twin wheels. In the 1970s, the Tyrrell P34 had four front wheels with a diameter of 10 inches.

  --- CHUNK 14 | Score: 0.282 ---
  Drivers must respect a minimum time established by the FIA at least once at each control post. Cars must not lap unnecessarily slowly, erratically or in a way that could be potentially dangerous. Anything that does not conform to those minimum times may be penalised. To end the virtual safety car period, teams will be notified and at any point between 10 and 15 seconds later the VSC signals on the panels will be switched off and green lights will be activated to restart the race immediately. 30 seconds later the green lights will be switched off.

This measure was applied following the death of Jules Bianchi at the 2014 Japanese Grand Prix, as he crashed into the recovery crane removing Adrian Sutil's single-seater at turn 7.


RACE AND QUALIFYING PENALTIES

Track marshals are responsible for penalising competitors. Penalties are usually carried out for actions such as: jumping the start, dangerous manoeuvres, ignoring flags, driving too fast in the pit lane, etc., depending on the severity or type of infringement.


THE GRAND PRIX EVENTS

Each year several Grand Prix events are organised in different parts of the world.

  The number of wheels on each car is limited to four, with no possibility of having three axles or twin wheels. In the 1970s, the Tyrrell P34 had four front wheels with a diameter of 10 inches.

For 2024, dry-weather tyres were reduced to 5 compounds, from C1 to C5 (with C1 being the most durable and slowest compound, and C5 the fastest with the most wear), eliminating the C0. Three compounds are chosen for each grand prix depending on the type of asphalt, which are named hard (white), medium (yellow) and soft (red). The most durable of the three is the hard, and the fastest is the soft. The tyres available for wet conditions are the intermediates (green) and the extreme wet tyres (blue). In this year the mandatory tyre system in qualifying that was trialled in 2023 was eliminated.

This proved to be the greatest technological advance since the introduction of rear-engined cars."].

  However, when Colin Chapman entered Formula 1 as a chassis designer and later as founder of the Lotus Team, British drivers enjoyed many more victories in the following decade. Among Jim Clark, Jackie Stewart, John Surtees, Jack Brabham, Graham Hill and Denny Hulme, drivers from British and Commonwealth teams won twelve world championships between 1962 and 1973.

In 1962, Lotus introduced a car with an aluminium monocoque chassis instead of the traditional tubular frame. This proved to be the greatest technological advance since the introduction of rear-engined cars. In 1968, Lotus placed the Imperial Tobacco logo on their cars, introducing tobacco sponsorship to the sport.

Aerodynamics slowly gained importance in car design from the appearance of aerodynamic wings in the late 1960s. Towards the end of the following decade, Lotus introduced a ground-effect bodywork that generated enormous downforce and allowed cars to travel at higher speeds in corners — although the concept had already been used in Jim Hall's Chaparral 2J in 1970.

  --- CHUNK 2 | Score: 0.334 ---
  FOCA threatened to establish a rival series, boycotted a Grand Prix and FISA withdrew its sanction from races. The result was the 1981 Concorde Agreement, which guaranteed technical stability as teams would receive reasonable notice of new regulations.

The development of electronic driver aids began during the 1980s. Lotus developed an active suspension system that first appeared during 1982 in the Lotus 91. In 1987, this system was perfected and driven to victory by Ayrton Senna at that year's Monaco Grand Prix. In the early 1990s other teams followed suit and semi-automatic gearboxes and traction control became a natural progression. The FIA, due to complaints that technology was determining race results more than driver skill, banned many of these aids for the 1994 season.

On track, McLaren and Williams dominated the 1980s and 1990s. Developed by Porsche, Honda and Mercedes-Benz, McLaren won sixteen championships during that period — seven constructors and nine drivers titles — while Williams used Ford, Honda and Renault engines to also win sixteen titles — nine constructors and seven drivers.

THE GARAGISTES (1950-1980)

The first major technological development occurred when the Cooper Car Company reintroduced rear-engined cars (following the pioneering Auto Union designs by Ferdinand Porsche in the 1930s), which evolved from the company's Formula 3 designs. Australian Jack Brabham, world champion in 1959, 1960 and 1966, soon demonstrated the superiority of the new design. By 1961, all regular competitors had switched to rear-engined cars. The Ferguson P99, with four-wheel drive, was the last front-engined Formula 1 car to participate in the World Championship, taking part only in the 1961 British Grand Prix.

The first British world champion was Mike Hawthorn, who drove a Ferrari to the title in 1958. However, when Colin Chapman entered Formula 1 as a chassis designer and later as founder of the Lotus Team, British drivers enjoyed many more victories in the following decade.

The 1952 and 1953 World Championships were run under Formula 2 regulations, with smaller and less powerful cars, due to the limited number of available Formula 1 cars. When the Formula 1 World Championship was reinstated, engines were limited to 2.5 litres. In 1954, Mercedes introduced the advanced W196, which featured innovations such as desmodromic valve timing and fuel injection, as well as a streamlined bodywork. Mercedes won the drivers championship two consecutive years (1954 and 1955) before withdrawing from all motorsport competition following the Le Mans disaster in 1955.

THE NEW ERA

After the pandemic, which delayed the biggest aerodynamic change in Formula 1 in 40 years from 2021 to 2022, it finally took place. The cars adopted the so-called ground effect, and many parts of the car were simplified, such as the wings, to reduce the turbulence each part produces and consequently the dirty air, which makes it harder for the following car to get close. This was done to improve the spectacle, allowing cars to follow each other more closely.

THE BIG BUSINESS (1981 ONWARDS)

From the 1970s, Bernie Ecclestone reorganised the management of Formula 1's commercial rights. Ecclestone is widely credited with transforming the sport into a multi-billion dollar business. When Ecclestone bought the Brabham team in 1971 he earned a seat in the Formula One Constructors Association (FOCA) and in 1978 became its president. Previously, circuit owners controlled team revenues and negotiated with each individually; however, Ecclestone convinced the teams to do so collectively through FOCA.

The formation of the Fédération Internationale du Sport Automobile (FISA) during 1979 triggered the FISA-FOCA dispute, during which FISA and its president Jean-Marie Balestre repeatedly clashed with FOCA over television revenues and technical regulations.

THE HYBRID ERA AND NEW OWNERS

THE EARLY RACES (1950-1958)

The first Grand Prix was held on 13 May 1950 at Silverstone circuit and was won by Italian driver Giuseppe Farina, who also claimed the 1950 championship after beating his teammate, Argentine Juan Manuel Fangio (both driving for Alfa Romeo). However, Fangio went on to win the title in 1951, 1954, 1955, 1956 and 1957. His streak was interrupted by Ferrari driver Alberto Ascari's back-to-back championships. This era also saw drivers such as British Stirling Moss, who despite competing regularly was never able to win the World Championship, which is why the English consider him "the greatest driver never to have won a world title".

The period was dominated by teams run by car manufacturers — Alfa Romeo, Ferrari, Maserati and Mercedes-Benz — who had competed before the war. In the early seasons, pre-Second World War cars such as the Alfa Romeo 159 were still being used.

  The scoring system has evolved significantly throughout the history of the championship. From 1950 to 1959, first place received 8 points, second place 6 points, third place 4 points, fourth place 3 points, fifth place 2 points, and an additional 1 point was awarded for the fastest lap of the race. During the 1960s, the points allocation remained the same as the previous decade for the top five positions, with first place earning 8 points, second 6 points, third 4 points, fourth 3 points and fifth 2 points, but with 1 point now awarded for sixth place, and the fastest lap bonus was removed. From 1961 to 1990, the structure remained largely the same, with 9 points for first place, 6 for second, 4 for third, 3 for fourth, 2 for fifth and 1 for sixth place. Between 1991 and 2002, first place was awarded 10 points, second place 6 points, third place 4 points, fourth place 3 points, fifth place 2 points and sixth place 1 point.

The presence of the safety car on the track is indicated by a sign with the letters SC (from the English "safety car") accompanied by a yellow flag.

  --- CHUNK 3 | Score: 0.389 ---
  Drivers must respect a minimum time established by the FIA at least once at each control post. Cars must not lap unnecessarily slowly, erratically or in a way that could be potentially dangerous. Anything that does not conform to those minimum times may be penalised. To end the virtual safety car period, teams will be notified and at any point between 10 and 15 seconds later the VSC signals on the panels will be switched off and green lights will be activated to restart the race immediately. 30 seconds later the green lights will be switched off.

This measure was applied following the death of Jules Bianchi at the 2014 Japanese Grand Prix, as he crashed into the recovery crane removing Adrian Sutil's single-seater at turn 7.


RACE AND QUALIFYING PENALTIES

Track marshals are responsible for penalising competitors. Penalties are usually carried out for actions such as: jumping the start, dangerous manoeuvres, ignoring flags, driving too fast in the pit lane, etc., depending on the severity or type of infringement.


THE GRAND PRIX EVENTS

Each year several Grand Prix events are organised in different parts of the world.

  --- CHUNK 4 | Score: 0.370 ---
  THE STEERING WHEEL

Due to the limited space in the cockpit of a Formula 1 single-seater and the degree of concentration and attention required to drive a Formula 1 car, the steering wheel of one of these single-seaters is not only the control mechanism for the car's steering, but also a complex interface with multiple electronic devices, both for providing information to the driver via displays, and for controlling the vehicle via buttons and dials that serve various functions.


TYRES

Tyres are designed to withstand forces much greater than conventional ones, so they are manufactured with nylon, polyester fibre and soft rubber compounds. During the race a tyre can withstand more than one tonne of aerodynamic load, lateral forces of 4g and longitudinal forces of up to 5g. The number of wheels on each car is limited to four, with no possibility of having three axles or twin wheels. In the 1970s, the Tyrrell P34 had four front wheels with a diameter of 10 inches.

  --- CHUNK 5 | Score: 0.352 ---
  This was done to improve the spectacle, allowing cars to follow each other more closely. In addition, an engine freeze has been implemented, where only reliability improvements can be made until the changes that will take place in 2026.


SPORT AND REGULATIONS

The Formula 1 regulations, drawn up by the Fédération Internationale de l'Automobile, are divided into two main parts. One of them, the Technical Regulations, refers to the technical conditions of the single-seaters, while the other, the Sporting Regulations, focuses on the conditions under which events must be held, as well as the scoring and penalty rules.


SCORING SYSTEM AND CHAMPIONSHIP ORDER

Over the years, the points system for drivers and constructors has varied, but it has always awarded more points to finishers at the top than at the bottom, except between 1950 and 1959 when the driver who set the fastest lap of the race also scored a point.

The scoring system has evolved significantly throughout the history of the championship.

  --- CHUNK 6 | Score: 0.337 ---
  FORMULA 1: THE PINNACLE OF MOTORSPORT

The FIA Formula One World Championship, better known as Formula 1, F1 or Formula One, is the premier international motorsport competition and the most popular and prestigious motor racing championship in the world. It is governed by the Fédération Internationale de l'Automobile (FIA). Since September 2016, following the acquisition of the Formula One Group, the American company Liberty Media has been responsible for managing and operating the championship.

Each race is called a Grand Prix and the tournament that groups them is the Formula One World Championship. Most of the racing circuits where the Grand Prix events are held are purpose-built racing tracks, although street circuits are also used, and road circuits were used in the past. The cars used are single-seaters featuring the latest available technology, always limited by technical regulations. Some improvements developed in Formula 1 have ended up being used in commercial vehicles, such as the disc brake.

The beginning of modern Formula 1 dates back to 1950, when teams such as Ferrari, Alfa Romeo and Maserati participated.

  --- CHUNK 7 | Score: 0.335 ---
  The flags used in Formula 1 carry specific meanings that all drivers must understand. The green flag indicates that the danger has passed and overtaking can resume. It can also be shown during the warm-up lap, at the beginning of a simultaneous practice session at all marshal posts when the start is announced, upon ignition of the starting lights, or when a car enters the garage safely. The red flag signals the stoppage of all practice sessions or the race, whether due to an accident or meteorological conditions. All drivers must immediately reduce their speed, stop if necessary and return to the boxes or to the place provided for in the event regulations. Overtaking is prohibited when the red flag is shown. This flag is displayed solely by order of the race director. All timing screens throughout the circuit turn red. If it occurs at the start of the race or at the restart without completing the lap or at least the first sector, all drivers must return to their original positions from the last lap without a red flag. The yellow flag indicates danger and overtaking is not permitted, and drivers must reduce their speed.

  --- CHUNK 8 | Score: 0.323 ---
  From 2019 onwards, the points allocation remains the same as the 2010 to 2018 period, with 25 points for first place down to 1 point for tenth place, and an additional 1 point is once again awarded for the fastest lap of the race, provided the driver who sets it finishes in the top ten.

The current points allocation in a Grand Prix is distributed as follows: the race winner receives 25 points; second place, 18; third, 15; fourth, 12; fifth, 10; sixth, 8; seventh, 6; eighth, 4; ninth, 2; and tenth, 1 point.


SAFETY SYSTEMS

Flags in Formula 1, and by extension in motorsport, are essential as they are the way in which track marshals communicate with drivers. Drivers must know and respect the different flags. The minimum dimensions are 60 cm x 80 cm, except for the red flag and the chequered flag which must be at least 80 x 100 cm.

The flags used in Formula 1 carry specific meanings that all drivers must understand.

  --- CHUNK 10 | Score: 0.302 ---
  The yellow flag indicates danger and overtaking is not permitted, and drivers must reduce their speed. It can be shown to drivers in two different ways: a single yellow flag means reduce speed, do not overtake and be prepared to change your line due to the presence of a danger on the edge of the track or on part of it; two yellow flags mean reduce speed, do not overtake and be prepared to change your line or even stop due to the presence of a danger that obstructs the track totally or partially. They are normally shown at the marshal post immediately before the danger, although in some cases more than one can be shown. The presence of this flag before the start, due to the impossibility of a driver starting, leads to the cancellation of the start. The safety car flag, which is a yellow flag with the letters SC or with a VSC marking on the panels, indicates that the safety car is intervening on the track. Drivers must reduce speed, not overtake, and even be prepared to stop and change their line as a danger totally or partially obstructs the track.
