  The number of wheels on each car is limited to four, with no possibility of having three axles or twin wheels. In the 1970s, the Tyrrell P34 had four front wheels with a diameter of 10 inches.

For 2024, dry-weather tyres were reduced to 5 compounds, from C1 to C5 (with C1 being the most durable and slowest compound, and C5 the fastest with the most wear), eliminating the C0. Three compounds are chosen for each grand prix depending on the type of asphalt, which are named hard (white), medium (yellow) and soft (red). The most durable of the three is the hard, and the fastest is the soft. The tyres available for wet conditions are the intermediates (green) and the extreme wet tyres (blue). In this year the mandatory tyre system in qualifying that was trialled in 2023 was eliminated.

  --- CHUNK 15 | Score: 0.229 ---
  The chequered black and white flag indicates the end of the race, or as the case may be, the end of each of the practice sessions of the competition.

Furthermore, since the 2009 British Grand Prix, the FIA Safety Light System has been regularly used, which represented a major advance as it is much easier for a driver to see a warning in the form of a light than in the form of a flag, especially in poor visibility conditions. Three-colour LEDs were added to the steering wheels of drivers — yellow, blue and red — which indicate whether any flag is being shown (yellow or red) or whether a blue flag is being shown to them (blue LED).

The safety car is an organisation vehicle that reports directly to Race Control. Its basic function is to neutralise races in order to group competitors together in the event of a serious incident or due to meteorological conditions. The presence of the safety car on the track is indicated by a sign with the letters SC (from the English "safety car") accompanied by a yellow flag.

THE EARLY RACES (1950-1958)

The first Grand Prix was held on 13 May 1950 at Silverstone circuit and was won by Italian driver Giuseppe Farina, who also claimed the 1950 championship after beating his teammate, Argentine Juan Manuel Fangio (both driving for Alfa Romeo). However, Fangio went on to win the title in 1951, 1954, 1955, 1956 and 1957. His streak was interrupted by Ferrari driver Alberto Ascari's back-to-back championships. This era also saw drivers such as British Stirling Moss, who despite competing regularly was never able to win the World Championship, which is why the English consider him "the greatest driver never to have won a world title".

The period was dominated by teams run by car manufacturers — Alfa Romeo, Ferrari, Maserati and Mercedes-Benz — who had competed before the war. In the early seasons, pre-Second World War cars such as the Alfa Romeo 159 were still being used.

  --- CHUNK 15 | Score: 0.259 ---
  The flags used in Formula 1 carry specific meanings that all drivers must understand. The green flag indicates that the danger has passed and overtaking can resume. It can also be shown during the warm-up lap, at the beginning of a simultaneous practice session at all marshal posts when the start is announced, upon ignition of the starting lights, or when a car enters the garage safely. The red flag signals the stoppage of all practice sessions or the race, whether due to an accident or meteorological conditions. All drivers must immediately reduce their speed, stop if necessary and return to the boxes or to the place provided for in the event regulations. Overtaking is prohibited when the red flag is shown. This flag is displayed solely by order of the race director. All timing screens throughout the circuit turn red. If it occurs at the start of the race or at the restart without completing the lap or at least the first sector, all drivers must return to their original positions from the last lap without a red flag. The yellow flag indicates danger and overtaking is not permitted, and drivers must reduce their speed.

The engine development and the 2017 regulatory change, which improved aerodynamic downforce, resulted in the single-seaters becoming the fastest in history.

The Formula One Group has been controlled since September 2016 by the American company Liberty Media, owned by John C. Malone. Bernie Ecclestone continued as chief executive of the company until 23 January 2017, after 39 years at the helm of Formula 1 business.


THE NEW ERA

After the pandemic, which delayed the biggest aerodynamic change in Formula 1 in 40 years from 2021 to 2022, it finally took place. The cars adopted the so-called ground effect, and many parts of the car were simplified, such as the wings, to reduce the turbulence each part produces and consequently the dirty air, which makes it harder for the following car to get close. This was done to improve the spectacle, allowing cars to follow each other more closely.

  --- CHUNK 3 | Score: 0.361 ---
  FORMULA 1: THE PINNACLE OF MOTORSPORT

The FIA Formula One World Championship, better known as Formula 1, F1 or Formula One, is the premier international motorsport competition and the most popular and prestigious motor racing championship in the world. It is governed by the Fédération Internationale de l'Automobile (FIA). Since September 2016, following the acquisition of the Formula One Group, the American company Liberty Media has been responsible for managing and operating the championship.

Each race is called a Grand Prix and the tournament that groups them is the Formula One World Championship. Most of the racing circuits where the Grand Prix events are held are purpose-built racing tracks, although street circuits are also used, and road circuits were used in the past. The cars used are single-seaters featuring the latest available technology, always limited by technical regulations. Some improvements developed in Formula 1 have ended up being used in commercial vehicles, such as the disc brake.

The beginning of modern Formula 1 dates back to 1950, when teams such as Ferrari, Alfa Romeo and Maserati participated.

  --- CHUNK 4 | Score: 0.345 ---
  Before the Second World War, rules had been established for Grand Prix competitions, which both cars and drivers had to follow. These rules, collectively known as the Formula, were not finalised until 1947, when the former AIACR was reorganised and renamed the Fédération Internationale de l'Automobile, known by the acronym FIA.

With its headquarters in Paris, at the end of the 1949 season it announced that for 1950 it would unite several national Grand Prix events to create a World Drivers Championship, so in 1950 the first Formula 1 championship was held. For economic reasons, in 1952 and 1953 competition still took place with Formula 2 cars, and the calendar continued to include several races that were not considered Grand Prix until 1983. A points system was established and a total of seven races were recognised as eligible for the World Championship.

  --- CHUNK 5 | Score: 0.342 ---
  THE STEERING WHEEL

Due to the limited space in the cockpit of a Formula 1 single-seater and the degree of concentration and attention required to drive a Formula 1 car, the steering wheel of one of these single-seaters is not only the control mechanism for the car's steering, but also a complex interface with multiple electronic devices, both for providing information to the driver via displays, and for controlling the vehicle via buttons and dials that serve various functions.


TYRES

Tyres are designed to withstand forces much greater than conventional ones, so they are manufactured with nylon, polyester fibre and soft rubber compounds. During the race a tyre can withstand more than one tonne of aerodynamic load, lateral forces of 4g and longitudinal forces of up to 5g. The number of wheels on each car is limited to four, with no possibility of having three axles or twin wheels. In the 1970s, the Tyrrell P34 had four front wheels with a diameter of 10 inches.

  --- CHUNK 6 | Score: 0.334 ---
  Meanwhile, Mercedes-Benz formed its own team in 2010, initially called Mercedes GP, while gradually withdrawing its financial support from McLaren.

During this period Red Bull and Sebastian Vettel dominated from 2010 to 2013, winning four consecutive drivers and constructors championships.


THE HYBRID ERA AND NEW OWNERS

In 2014, Formula 1 underwent a major regulatory change, replacing V8 engines with turbocharged 1.6-litre V6 units. The single-seaters now feature a hybrid power unit, adding an energy recovery system. Honda joined Mercedes, Ferrari and Renault in 2015 as an engine supplier to McLaren, making them the only four manufacturers of this era.

Lewis Hamilton and Mercedes dominated the beginning of the hybrid era. His teammate Nico Rosberg won in 2016, but from 2017 Hamilton won four more consecutive titles, equalling Michael Schumacher's record of 7 world titles. In 2021, one of the most intense title battles took place between Hamilton and Max Verstappen, a duel that the Dutchman would ultimately win.

  --- CHUNK 7 | Score: 0.330 ---
  The beginning of modern Formula 1 dates back to 1950, when teams such as Ferrari, Alfa Romeo and Maserati participated. Some were replaced by new ones such as McLaren, Williams, Red Bull, and others that returned like Mercedes have won the Formula One World Constructors Championship multiple times. Drivers must hold an FIA Super Licence to compete, which is obtained through results in other championships.


BACKGROUND

Grand Prix racing has its roots in motor racing events that originated in France in 1894. Initially they were individual events, unconnected and on dirt roads, with virtually no limitations. From 1927 to 1934, the number of races considered Grand Prix grew to reach eighteen in 1934, the maximum before the Second World War.


CREATION OF THE CHAMPIONSHIP

In 1945, immediately after the Second World War, there were only four races. Before the Second World War, rules had been established for Grand Prix competitions, which both cars and drivers had to follow.

  --- CHUNK 8 | Score: 0.328 ---
  From 2019 onwards, the points allocation remains the same as the 2010 to 2018 period, with 25 points for first place down to 1 point for tenth place, and an additional 1 point is once again awarded for the fastest lap of the race, provided the driver who sets it finishes in the top ten.

The current points allocation in a Grand Prix is distributed as follows: the race winner receives 25 points; second place, 18; third, 15; fourth, 12; fifth, 10; sixth, 8; seventh, 6; eighth, 4; ninth, 2; and tenth, 1 point.


SAFETY SYSTEMS

Flags in Formula 1, and by extension in motorsport, are essential as they are the way in which track marshals communicate with drivers. Drivers must know and respect the different flags. The minimum dimensions are 60 cm x 80 cm, except for the red flag and the chequered flag which must be at least 80 x 100 cm.

The flags used in Formula 1 carry specific meanings that all drivers must understand.

  --- CHUNK 9 | Score: 0.319 ---
  The aerodynamic forces pressing the cars to the track were so great, up to 5g, that it was necessary to increase the stiffness of the suspension springs so they had less influence on the chassis ride height, leaving the suspension almost rigid.


THE BIG BUSINESS (1981 ONWARDS)

From the 1970s, Bernie Ecclestone reorganised the management of Formula 1's commercial rights. Ecclestone is widely credited with transforming the sport into a multi-billion dollar business. When Ecclestone bought the Brabham team in 1971 he earned a seat in the Formula One Constructors Association (FOCA) and in 1978 became its president. Previously, circuit owners controlled team revenues and negotiated with each individually; however, Ecclestone convinced the teams to do so collectively through FOCA.

The formation of the Fédération Internationale du Sport Automobile (FISA) during 1979 triggered the FISA-FOCA dispute, during which FISA and its president Jean-Marie Balestre repeatedly clashed with FOCA over television revenues and technical regulations.

  --- CHUNK 10 | Score: 0.313 ---
  FOCA threatened to establish a rival series, boycotted a Grand Prix and FISA withdrew its sanction from races. The result was the 1981 Concorde Agreement, which guaranteed technical stability as teams would receive reasonable notice of new regulations.

The development of electronic driver aids began during the 1980s. Lotus developed an active suspension system that first appeared during 1982 in the Lotus 91. In 1987, this system was perfected and driven to victory by Ayrton Senna at that year's Monaco Grand Prix. In the early 1990s other teams followed suit and semi-automatic gearboxes and traction control became a natural progression. The FIA, due to complaints that technology was determining race results more than driver skill, banned many of these aids for the 1994 season.

On track, McLaren and Williams dominated the 1980s and 1990s. Developed by Porsche, Honda and Mercedes-Benz, McLaren won sixteen championships during that period — seven constructors and nine drivers titles — while Williams used Ford, Honda and Renault engines to also win sixteen titles — nine constructors and seven drivers.

  --- CHUNK 11 | Score: 0.311 ---
  The number of wheels on each car is limited to four, with no possibility of having three axles or twin wheels. In the 1970s, the Tyrrell P34 had four front wheels with a diameter of 10 inches.

For 2024, dry-weather tyres were reduced to 5 compounds, from C1 to C5 (with C1 being the most durable and slowest compound, and C5 the fastest with the most wear), eliminating the C0. Three compounds are chosen for each grand prix depending on the type of asphalt, which are named hard (white), medium (yellow) and soft (red). The most durable of the three is the hard, and the fastest is the soft. The tyres available for wet conditions are the intermediates (green) and the extreme wet tyres (blue). In this year the mandatory tyre system in qualifying that was trialled in 2023 was eliminated.

  --- CHUNK 12 | Score: 0.309 ---
  Schumacher's run ended on 25 September 2005, when Renault driver Fernando Alonso became the youngest Formula 1 champion at that time. During 2006, Renault and Alonso won both titles again. Schumacher retired at the end of 2006 after sixteen years in Formula 1, but came out of retirement for the 2010 season, racing for the newly formed Mercedes team for three seasons.

During this period the FIA frequently changed championship rules with the intention of improving on-track action and reducing costs. Team orders, legal since the championship began in 1950, were banned in 2002 following several incidents in which teams openly manipulated race results. The most famous case was the 2002 Austrian Grand Prix involving Ferrari, but they would become legal again for the 2011 season.


THE DECLINE OF THE MANUFACTURERS

In the late 2000s, as a consequence of the economic crisis, several teams withdrew from F1: Honda and Super Aguri at the end of 2008, and BMW and Toyota at the end of 2009.

  --- CHUNK 13 | Score: 0.293 ---
  Mercedes won the drivers championship two consecutive years (1954 and 1955) before withdrawing from all motorsport competition following the Le Mans disaster in 1955.


THE GARAGISTES (1950-1980)

The first major technological development occurred when the Cooper Car Company reintroduced rear-engined cars (following the pioneering Auto Union designs by Ferdinand Porsche in the 1930s), which evolved from the company's Formula 3 designs. Australian Jack Brabham, world champion in 1959, 1960 and 1966, soon demonstrated the superiority of the new design. By 1961, all regular competitors had switched to rear-engined cars. The Ferguson P99, with four-wheel drive, was the last front-engined Formula 1 car to participate in the World Championship, taking part only in the 1961 British Grand Prix.

The first British world champion was Mike Hawthorn, who drove a Ferrari to the title in 1958. However, when Colin Chapman entered Formula 1 as a chassis designer and later as founder of the Lotus Team, British drivers enjoyed many more victories in the following decade.

  --- CHUNK 14 | Score: 0.289 ---
  In the early seasons, pre-Second World War cars such as the Alfa Romeo 159 were still being used. They featured front-mounted engines, either 1.5-litre supercharged or 4.5-litre naturally aspirated, and treaded tyres. The 1952 and 1953 World Championships were run under Formula 2 regulations, with smaller and less powerful cars, due to the limited number of available Formula 1 cars. When the Formula 1 World Championship was reinstated, engines were limited to 2.5 litres. In 1954, Mercedes introduced the advanced W196, which featured innovations such as desmodromic valve timing and fuel injection, as well as a streamlined bodywork. Mercedes won the drivers championship two consecutive years (1954 and 1955) before withdrawing from all motorsport competition following the Le Mans disaster in 1955.

  --- CHUNK 15 | Score: 0.285 ---
  Drivers must respect a minimum time established by the FIA at least once at each control post. Cars must not lap unnecessarily slowly, erratically or in a way that could be potentially dangerous. Anything that does not conform to those minimum times may be penalised. To end the virtual safety car period, teams will be notified and at any point between 10 and 15 seconds later the VSC signals on the panels will be switched off and green lights will be activated to restart the race immediately. 30 seconds later the green lights will be switched off.

This measure was applied following the death of Jules Bianchi at the 2014 Japanese Grand Prix, as he crashed into the recovery crane removing Adrian Sutil's single-seater at turn 7.


RACE AND QUALIFYING PENALTIES

Track marshals are responsible for penalising competitors. Penalties are usually carried out for actions such as: jumping the start, dangerous manoeuvres, ignoring flags, driving too fast in the pit lane, etc., depending on the severity or type of infringement.


THE GRAND PRIX EVENTS

Each year several Grand Prix events are organised in different parts of the world.
